نکاح انہوں نے اس سے نکاح صحیح کا ارادہ کیا ہے بہر حال جب مولیٰ نے ان میں سے ایک کا نکاح کر دیا ہو نکاح فاسد کے ساتھ تو اس کے لئے دوسری سے وطی کرنا مباح نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہ شوہر اس کے ساتھ نکاح فاسد میں وطی کرے اس لئے کہ اس پر عدت واجب ہوگی اور تحریم کے سلسلے میں عدت نکاح صحیح سے مثل ہے اور اگر ان دونوں میں سے ایک سے اس نے وطی کی ہو تو اس کے لئے موطوء ہ سے وطی حلال ہے نہ کہ دوسری سے اس لئے کہ وہ دوسری کی وطی سے جمع کرنے والا ہوگا نہ کہ موطورہ کی وطی سے اور ہرایسی دو عورتیں جن کے درمیان نکاح میں جمع کرنا جائز نہ ہو وہ ان تمام صورتوں میں جن کو ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے دو بہنوں کے درجہ میں ہیں تشریح متن میں جو امام محمد کا قول آیا تھا او نکاح مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ اس سے مراد نکاح صحیح ہے اور اگر مولیٰ نے ایک باندی کا نکاح دوسرے سے کیا ہوا لیکن وہ نکاح فاسد ہو تو مولی کے لئے اس کی بہن سے وطی کرنا حلال نہیں ہے البتہ اگر شوہر نے نکاح فاسد میں اس سے وطی کر لی ہو تو اب مولی کے لئے دوسری باندی خلال ہو جائے گی کیونکہ دخول کی وجہ سے اس پر عدت واجب ہوگی اور جب عدت واجب ہوگئی تو معتدہ اپنے مولیٰ پر حرام ہوگئی اور جب وہ حرام ہو گئی تو اب اس کی بہن سے وطی کرنا حلال ہو گیا بالفاظ دیگر جس طریقہ سے نکاح صحیح سے مولیٰ کے لئے حرمت ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اس طرح عدت سے بھی حرمت ثابت ہو جائے گی اور اگر مولیٰ نے صرف ایک سے وطی کی ہو تو بس اس سے وطی کرتا رہے دوسری سے نہ کرے کیونکہ جمع بین الا حتمین دوسری سے وطی کرنے سے لازم آئے گا موطوء ہ سے بار بار وطی کرنے سے جمع بین الا تین لازم نہیں آئے گا ایسی دو عورتیں جن کو ایک ساتھ نکاح میں جمع کرنا جائز نہیں ہے ایسی دو باندیوں کو مولی ولی میں بھی جمع نہیں کر سکتا اور نہ دوائی ولی میں مثلاً خالہ ، بھانجی، پھوپھی بھتیجی، اختین نکاح میں جمع کر سکتا اسی طرح اگر یہ ایک موٹی کی ملک میں جمع ہو جائیں تو دونوں سے وطی اور دوائی نہیں کر سکتا۔ تنبیہ نکاح فاسد میں دخول کے بعد وجوب عدت کی وجہ سے مولیٰ کے لئے دوسری باندی سے ولی کرنا حلال ہو گیا لیکن جب اس کی عدت گذرگئی تو حرمت لوٹ کر آ جائے گی ۔ لہذا ان دونوں میں سے کسی سے وطی نہیں کریگا یہاں تک کہ ایک کو بیچ دے یا نکاح کر دے۔ ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۲۴۳ ج ۵
Book of Disliked Acts. Now the other [sister] will also be halal, because when one became haram, then he will not be combining two sisters by having intercourse with the other. By 'milk' is meant milk al-yamin (right hand ownership). And his statement 'by ownership' he meant milk al-yamin, so it includes making lawful ownership by all its causes, sale or otherwise. And making someone own a share is like making them own the whole, because intercourse becomes forbidden by it. And similarly, freeing part of one of them is like freeing all of her. And similarly, kitaba (contract of manumission) is like freeing in this, due to the prohibition of intercourse being established by all that. Translation: And the statement of Muhammad 'by ownership' he meant milk al-yamin, so it includes making someone an owner by all means of ownership, sale or otherwise. And making someone own a share is like making them own the whole, because intercourse becomes forbidden by it. And similarly, freeing part of one of them is like freeing all of her. And similarly, kitaba is like freeing in this matter due to the establishment of the prohibition of intercourse by all that. Explanation: By 'milk' Imam Muhammad meant that another person becomes the owner of this slave-girl, which is expressed as milk al-yamin. So whatever means of ownership are adopted, all are equal: whether sale, gift, charity, or ... And a slave-girl shared between two men, it is not halal for either of them to have intercourse with her. Therefore, if the master makes someone own part of one of the two slave-girls, since she has become haram upon the master, now intercourse with her sister and ... is halal. Similarly, if he freed part of one, still intercourse with the other sister is halal. Similarly, if he made one a kitaba, the same ruling, because in all these cases prohibition is established. And when prohibition is established, then having intercourse with her sister does not necessitate combining the sisters. Two sisters - making one a pledge, lease, or mudabbara does not make the other halal. And their pledge, lease, and tadbir do not make the other halal because they do not remove her from his ownership. Translation: And making one of them a pledge, and leasing one, and making one mudabbara does not make the other halal, because she does not leave his ownership by these acts. Explanation: If the master pawned one of them to someone, since the pawned one does not leave the master's ownership, the other slave-girl is not halal. Similarly, if he leased one, she does not leave his ownership, so intercourse with the other sister is not halal. Similarly, if he made one mudabbara, since the mudabbara does not leave the master's ownership, intercourse with her sister is not halal. The master made a fasid marriage of one slave-girl to someone: for the master, intercourse with her sister is permissible. And his statement 'or marriage' he meant valid marriage. But if he married one of them in a fasid marriage, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with the other unless the husband has intercourse with her in it, because iddah becomes obligatory upon her, and iddah is like a valid marriage in prohibition. And if he had intercourse with one of them, then intercourse with the one he had intercourse with is halal, not the other, because he would be combining [the sisters] by having intercourse with the other, not by having intercourse with the one he already had. And every two women between whom combination in marriage is not permissible are, in all the cases we have mentioned, like two sisters. Explanation: In the text, there was the statement of Imam Muhammad 'or marriage'. The author says that by this he means valid marriage. And if the master married one slave-girl to someone but that marriage was fasid, then for the master, intercourse with her sister is not halal. However, if the husband had intercourse with her in the fasid marriage, then for the master the other slave-girl becomes halal, because due to consummation iddah becomes obligatory upon her. And when iddah becomes obligatory, the woman in iddah becomes haram upon her master. And when she becomes haram, then intercourse with her sister becomes halal. In other words, just as prohibition is established for the master through a valid marriage, similarly prohibition is established through iddah. And if the master only had intercourse with one, then he may continue having intercourse with her and not with the other, because combining the two negatives would be necessitated by having intercourse with the other, not by repeatedly having intercourse with the one he had. Two women whom it is not permissible to combine in marriage, the master cannot combine them in intercourse, nor in ... for example, maternal aunt, niece, paternal aunt, brother's daughter, two sisters in marriage. Similarly, if they come together in the ownership of one master, he cannot have intercourse with both nor ... . Note: After consummation in a fasid marriage, due to the obligation of iddah, for the master it becomes halal to have intercourse with the other slave-girl. But when her iddah passes, the prohibition returns and will remain as long as he does not marry her off to someone. Similarly, if the master married one of them, then the husband divorced her and her iddah passed, the previous prohibition returns. Therefore, he will not have intercourse with either of them until he sells one or marries her off. See Shami, vol. 5, p. 243.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Karahiyya of Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with the rulings concerning a master who owns two slave-girls who are sisters. The central issue is the prohibition of having intercourse with both sisters (jama' bayn al-ukhtayn) in ownership, analogous to the prohibition in marriage. The commentator explains that if the master takes an action that permanently removes one sister from his ownership or makes her forbidden to him for other reasons, the other sister becomes permissible. Actions that achieve this include: transferring full or partial ownership to another person (by sale, gift, charity, etc.), freeing part or all of one sister, or entering into a kitaba contract (a contract of gradual manumission). In all these cases, the sister becomes haram for the master, thus the other is halal. However, actions that do not remove the slave-girl from his ownership—such as pledging her as collateral (rahn), leasing her (ijarah), or making her mudabbara (promising freedom upon the master's death)—do not make the other sister halal. The passage then discusses the case where the master marries one sister to someone else. If the marriage is valid, the sister becomes haram for the master, and the other becomes halal. If the marriage is fasid (irregular), it does not immediately make the other halal unless the husband consummates the marriage, which then obligates iddah (waiting period). During iddah, the slave-girl is considered forbidden to the master just as in a valid marriage, so the other sister becomes halal. Once the iddah ends, the prohibition on the first sister is lifted (since she returns to the master's ownership without marriage), and the prohibition on combining returns, so the master must again avoid having intercourse with both. The note emphasizes that this prohibition persists until he sells one or marries her off. The general principle derived is that any two women whom it is forbidden to combine in marriage (e.g., sisters, aunt and niece) are also forbidden to combine in intercourse when owned as slaves.

Key Terms
ملک یمین (milk al-yamin): Ownership by right hand, referring to the ownership of a slave-girl.
جمع بین الأختین (jama' bayn al-ukhtayn): The act of having intercourse with two sisters, which is prohibited in both marriage and slave ownership.
وطی (wati'): Sexual intercourse, specifically the act that is subject to rulings of permissibility and prohibition.
کتابة (kitaba): A contract of manumission where the slave pays the master in installments and becomes free upon completion.
مدبرة (mudabbara): A slave-girl who has been promised freedom upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master owns two sister slave-girls. He makes one a mudabbara. Which of the following is true?
A) He may now have intercourse with the other sister because the mudabbara is forbidden to him.
B) He may not have intercourse with the other sister because the mudabbara remains in his ownership.
C) He must sell the mudabbara before he can have intercourse with the other.
D) He may have intercourse with both as long as he does not combine them in one session.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of combining two sister slave-girls in intercourse is lifted only when one sister is permanently removed from the master's ownership or made forbidden to him, either by transfer, manumission, kitaba, valid marriage, or iddah after consummation of a fasid marriage.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
Master's Intercourse with Slave Sister After Fasid Nikah
Explains master's options after fasid nikah of his slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules to determine when a master may lawfully have intercourse with the sister of his slave woman when the first slave woman has been married in a fasid nikah.

Core Idea
Fasid nikah with consummation creates iddah, which prohibits the married slave and permits her sister.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a subtle case: a master owns two slave women who cannot be combined in marriage (e.g., sisters). He marries one to another man with a fasid nikah. When may he approach the other? The key is that a consummated fasid nikah triggers iddah, which acts like a valid marriage in creating prohibitions. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof via analogy to sisters, and the practical implications for a master's options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Start with the two slave women who are like sisters (i.e., cannot be combined in marriage). The master marries one of them to another man with a fasid nikah. Determine whether the husband has consummated that marriage.
2
Apply the ruling for each subcase
If the husband has NOT consummated, the master may NOT have intercourse with the sister because the married slave remains permissible to the master (no iddah), so combining her and her sister is prohibited. If the husband HAS consummated, the married slave enters iddah, making her prohibited to the master like a married woman; then her sister becomes permissible.
The ruling also covers the case where the master himself has intercourse with one slave: then only that one is permissible, not the other, because repeated intercourse with the same woman doesn't constitute combining.
3
Identify the proof structure
The Hidayah uses qiyas: the original case (asl) is the prohibition of combining two sisters. The effective cause ('illa) is the relationship that makes combination in marriage unlawful. This 'illa applies to any two women who cannot be married together (e.g., aunt-niece). The far' is any such pair owned by the master. Thus, the same prohibition of combination applies to intercourse via ownership.
The text explicitly states 'they are like two sisters' (بمنزلة الاختين), indicating the analogy.
4
Note the temporary nature of the permission
The tanbih (note) clarifies that the permission for the sister is temporary. Once the iddah of the married slave ends (e.g., after three menses), the prohibition returns unless she is married again to someone else or sold.
Also if the husband divorces and iddah ends, the same return of prohibition applies.

طلاق دیدی اور اس کی عدت گزر گئی تو حرمت سابقہ لوٹ کر آ جائے گی ۔ لہذا ان دونوں میں سے کسی سے وطی نہیں کریگا یہاں تک کہ ایک کو بیچ دے یا نکاح کر دے۔ ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۲۴۳ ج ۵
If a master marries one of his slave women with a fasid nikah, then for the master it is permissible to have intercourse with her sister. And his saying 'or nikah' means he intended a valid nikah. However, if the master marries one of them with a fasid nikah, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with the other, unless the husband has intercourse with her in that fasid nikah, because iddah becomes obligatory upon her, and iddah is like a valid nikah in terms of prohibition. And if he has intercourse with one of them, then intercourse with the one with whom he had intercourse is permissible for him, not with the other, because he would be combining [between them] by intercourse with the other, not by intercourse with the one already penetranted. And every two women between whom combination in marriage is not permissible are, in all the cases we have mentioned, like two sisters. Translation and commentary: [Sahib's] saying 'or nikah' means he intended a valid nikah. However, when the master marries one of them with a fasid nikah, then for him intercourse with the other is not permissible unless the husband has intercourse with her in the fasid nikah, because iddah becomes obligatory upon her due to penetration, and when iddah becomes obligatory, the woman in iddah becomes forbidden to her master, and when she becomes forbidden, then intercourse with her sister becomes permissible. In other words, just as prohibition is established for the master through a valid nikah, so too prohibition is established through iddah. And if the master has intercourse with only one, then he may continue intercourse with that one and not with the other, because combining between the two would necessitate by intercourse with the other, whereas repeated intercourse with the one does not necessitate combination. Such two women whom it is not permissible to combine in marriage, a master also cannot combine them in ownership, nor in both ownership and marriage, e.g. maternal aunt and niece, paternal aunt and niece, two sisters – he cannot combine them in marriage. Similarly, if they are combined in the ownership of one person, he cannot have intercourse with both nor marry both. Note: After penetration in fasid nikah, due to the obligation of iddah, intercourse with the other slave woman became permissible for the master, but when her iddah passes, the prohibition returns, and this prohibition remains until she is married to someone else. Similarly, if the master married one of them, then the husband divorced her and her iddah passed, the previous prohibition returns. Therefore, he will not have intercourse with either of them until he sells one of them or marries her off. See Shami, vol.5, p.243.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Karahiyah explains the ruling when a master owns two slave women who are so related that they cannot both be married to one man at the same time (e.g., two sisters, aunt and niece). The master wishes to have intercourse with one of them, but the other is already married to someone else. The text clarifies the situation based on whether the marriage is valid or fasid and whether consummation occurred. The key principle is that iddah creates prohibition just like a valid nikah. The text analogizes such women to two sisters in all the mentioned cases. The tanbih adds that the prohibition is temporary; after the iddah ends, the woman becomes permissible again to the master unless she is married again.

Key Terms
نکاح فاسد (nikah fasid): A marriage contract that is defective in its conditions (e.g., lacking witnesses) but not batil. In Hanafi law, such a contract can produce legal effects like iddah if consummated.
عدت (iddah): A waiting period after separation or death, during which a woman cannot remarry. Here, it is the period after consummation of a fasid nikah.
بمنزلة الاختين (bi-manzilat al-ukhtayn): Treated legally like two sisters in terms of prohibition of combination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a fasid nikah create an iddah that makes the married slave impermissible for her master?
A) Immediately upon the contract.
B) Only after the husband consummates the marriage.
C) Only if the marriage is later declared batil.
D) Never; fasid nikah has no legal effects.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of a master's intercourse with a slave woman's sister depends on whether the first slave is in iddah from a consummated fasid nikah, which prohibits her and thus permits her sister by operation of the 'like sisters' analogy.

Relying on News of Husband's Death or Divorce: The Hanafi Rule of Qati' Tari
Ruling with explanation of qati' tari vs muqarin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for qati' tari and qati' muqarin to determine when a woman may rely on news of her husband's death or divorce and remarry.

Core Idea
A single trustworthy witness suffices when news arises after the contract (qati' tari); otherwise, two witnesses are needed (qati' muqarin).

In Hanafi fiqh, a woman whose husband is absent may receive news of his death or divorce. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir and the key distinction between a definitive proof that arises later (qati' tari) versus one present from the start (qati' muqarin). Understanding this distinction is essential for correct application of the evidentiary rules in marriage matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation and the news
The woman's husband is absent. She receives news of death or triple divorce from a trustworthy person, or from an untrustworthy person with a written document.
2
Determine the type of qati'
Is the definitive proof (qati') tari or muqarin? Tari means the evidence appears after the marriage contract and does not invalidate it retroactively. Muqarin means the evidence existed at the time of the contract, making it void from the start.
3
Apply the evidentiary rule
For qati' tari, a single trustworthy informant suffices, or even an untrustworthy one if supported by dominant opinion (ghalabat al-zann) after investigation. For qati' muqarin, two just witnesses are required.
4
Derive the ruling
In the case described, the qati' is tari because the news came after the marriage. Therefore, the woman may observe iddah and remarry based on the news, provided the conditions (trustworthiness or dominant opinion) are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عورت کا شوہر غائب ہو عورت کو ثقہ نے خبر دی کہ تیرا شوہر مر گیا یا اس نے تمہیں تین طلاقیں دیدیں یا وہ ثقہ نہیں ہے لیکن شوہر کی جانب سے طلاق نامہ کی تحریر پیش کرتا ہے عورت تحریر نہیں جانتی لیکن تحری کے بعد معلوم ہوا کہ یہ سچا ہے تو عورت اس کے قول پر اعتماد کر سکتی ہے
قال ولو ان امرأة اخبرها ثقة ان زوجها الغائب مات عنها او طلقها ثلثا وكان غير ثقة واتاها بكتاب من زوجها بالطلاق ولا تدرى انه كتابه ام لا الا ان اكبر رايها انه حق يعني بعد التحرى فلا بأس بان تعتد ثم تتزوج لان القاطع طار ولا منازع
ترجمہ محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا کہ اگر کسی عورت کو کسی ثقہ نے خبر دی کہ اس کا غائب شوہر مر گیا یا اس کو تین طلاقیں دیدی یا وہ غیر ثقہ ہو اور اس کے پاس اس کے شوہر کا خط طلاق کے سلسلہ میں لایا ( طلاق نامہ ) اور عورت نہیں جانتی کہ یہ اس کا خط ہے یا نہیں مگر اس کا غالب گمان یہ ہے کہ یہ سچ ہے یعنی تحری کے بعد تو کوئی حرج نہیں کہ وہ عورت عدت گزارے پھر نکاح کرے اس لئے کہ قاطع نکاح ظاہری اور کوئی معارض نہیں ہے۔ تشریح ایک عورت کا شوہر غائب ہے کسی ثقہ نے اس کو خبر دی کہ تیرا شوہر نمٹ لیا ( مر گیا ) یا اس نے تجھے تین طلاقیں دیدی یا وہ خود تو ثقہ نہیں ہے لیکن وہ شوہر کی جانب سے طلاق نامہ کی تحریری عورت کو پیش کرتا ہے اور عورت یہ نہیں جانتی کہ یہ اس کی تحریر ہے یا نہیں لیکن تحری کے بعد اس کا ظن غالب ہو گیا کہ مخبر سچا ہے تو اس کے لئے جائز ہے کہ اس کی بات پر اعتماد کرے اور عدت گزار کر اپنا دوسرا نکاح کر لے کیوں، اس لئے کہ اس کی بات کا کوئی معارض نہیں نیز یہ قاطع طاری ہے نہ کہ مقارن ۔
سوال اس کا کیا مطلب ہے؟
جواب .. قاطع دو قسم کا ہوتا ہے
-1- طاری
-2- مقارن
طاری کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ یہ قاطع پہلے سے نہیں بعد میں عارض ہوا ہے اور مقارن کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ قاطع عقد پہلے ہی سے موجود ہے گویا عقد سرے سے فاسد ہے اور اصول یہ مقرر ہے کہ قاطع طاری میں ایک ثقہ معتبر ہوتا ہے یا غیر ثقہ کا جب کہ ظن غالب سے اس کی تائید ہو جائے ، اور قاطع مقارن کے اندر نصاب شہادت مع عدالت کے ضروری ہے ورنہ ایک آدمی کا قول اس میں معتبر نہ ہوگا۔
When a woman's husband is absent, if a trustworthy person informs her that her husband died or gave her three divorces, or if the informant is not trustworthy and brings a written divorce document from her husband, and she does not know whether it is his writing, but after investigation her dominant opinion is that it is true, then she can rely on his statement, observe iddah, and remarry. Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a trustworthy person informs a woman that her absent husband died or gave her three divorces, or if he is not trustworthy and brings a letter from her husband about divorce, and she does not know whether it is his letter, but her dominant opinion after investigation is that it is true, then there is no harm in her observing iddah and then marrying, because the definitive proof is intervening and there is no opponent. Explanation: ... Question: What does this mean? Answer: Qati' is of two types: tari and muqarin. Tari means it occurs later, not from the beginning. Muqarin means it is present from the start, so the contract is invalid from the outset. The principle is that in qati' tari, one trustworthy person is acceptable, or even an untrustworthy person if supported by dominant opinion. But in qati' muqarin, the full evidence of two witnesses with integrity is required; otherwise one person's word is not acceptable.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on when a woman can rely on news of her husband's death or divorce to remarry. The author, commenting on the matn from al-Jami' al-Saghir, explains that the key factor is the nature of the definitive proof (qati'). If the qati' is 'tari' (intervening after the contract), a single trustworthy informant suffices, or even an untrustworthy one if supported by dominant opinion. If the qati' is 'muqarin' (existing from the start of the contract), the full evidence of two just witnesses is required because the contract is fundamentally invalid. The ruling highlights the Hanafi distinction between procedural and substantive certainty.

Key Terms
ثقہ (thiqah): A trustworthy, reliable person whose statement is accepted in legal matters.
ظن غالب (ghalabat al-zann): Dominant opinion; a strong subjective probability that something is true.
قاطع طاری (qati' tari): A definitive proof that arises after the inception of the contract, not present at the time of the contract.
قاطع مقارن (qati' muqarin): A definitive proof that is concurrent with the contract, making it invalid from the start.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, when can a woman act on news of her husband's death from an untrustworthy person?
A) Always
B) Never
C) Only if she receives a written letter and after investigation her dominant opinion supports it
D) Only if two trustworthy witnesses inform her

Key Takeaway
When a definitive proof arises after the contract (qati' tari), a single trustworthy informant or even an untrustworthy one with dominant opinion suffices for a woman to act on news of her husband's death or divorce and remarry.

Woman's Statement of Divorce and Iddah: Sufficiency for Remarriage
Ruling: woman's statement alone suffices for remarriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a woman's statement about her divorce and iddah completion is sufficient for a man to marry her, and identify the condition of truthfulness.

Core Idea
A woman's word suffices for remarriage when no opponent exists, provided she is likely truthful.

This lesson addresses a practical situation: a woman tells a man that her husband divorced her and her waiting period (iddah) has ended. Can the man marry her based solely on her statement? The Hanafi school gives a clear ruling, along with an important condition. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a man marrying a woman based on her statement about her divorce and iddah. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) and there is no harm (la ba's).
2
Understand the reason
The reason given is that there is no opposing party (mu'arid). In the absence of a claim against her statement, her word is taken as credible.
This is based on principle of qati' tari—giving weight to apparent evidence when no counter-evidence exists.
3
Note the condition
The ruling applies only when the man's dominant opinion (zann ghalib) is that she is truthful. If he doubts her truthfulness, he should not rely on her statement.
This condition prevents misuse of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عورت نے ایک شخص کو کہا کہ میرے شوہر نے مجھے طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گذر گئی تو شخص نکاح کرسکتا ہے
وكذا لو قالت لرجل طلقني زوجي وانقضت عدتى فلا بأس أن يتزوجها ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر عورت نے کہا کسی شخص سے کہ میرے شوہر نے مجھ کو طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گزرگئی تو کوئی حرج نہیں کہ وہ اس سے نکاح کرے۔ تشریح کیوں کہ کوئی معارض نہیں ہے لیکن اس کا یہ حکم جب ہے کہ اس کا ظن غالب یہ ہو کہ یہ بچی ہے، محمد یوسف عفرلہ ۔
A woman said to a man, 'My husband divorced me and my iddah passed,' then the man can marry her. And similarly, if a woman says to a man, 'My husband divorced me and my iddah passed,' there is no harm in him marrying her. Explanation: Because there is no opponent, but this ruling applies when her dominant opinion is that she is truthful.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: if a woman informs a man that her husband divorced her and her iddah has ended, the man can marry her. The reason given is that there is no opposing claim. However, the condition is that the woman must be truthful in her statement. This is based on the same principle of qati' tari, where her word alone suffices since there is no evidence to the contrary.

Key Terms
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A woman's statement about her divorce and iddah is accepted for remarriage when there is no opposing claim and the man believes she is truthful.

Accepting a Slave Girl's Claim of Emancipation
Ruling: slave girl's word of freedom is accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding a slave girl's claim of emancipation and identify the key principle (qati' tari) that makes her word acceptable.

Core Idea
A slave girl's claim of freedom is accepted without witnesses when the proof of slavery is intervening (tari).

This lesson presents a specific Hanafi ruling about a slave girl who claims her master freed her. The acceptance of her statement hinges on a critical distinction between types of definitive proof (qati'): intervening (tari) and concurrent (muqarin). Understanding this principle clarifies when her word alone suffices for legal validity, such as in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
First, pinpoint the act being ruled upon: a slave girl claims her master freed her. The Hanafi ruling is that her statement is accepted, and it is permissible to marry her based solely on her word.
This is a straightforward application of the principle; no witnesses are required.
2
Understand the key term: qati' al-riqqiyyah
Qati' means definitive proof. In this context, it refers to proof of slavery status. The Hanafi school divides it into two types: tari (intervening, meaning the proof is only relevant to the past, and at the time of the contract she is free) and muqarin (concurrent, meaning she is still a slave at the time of the claim). Only when the proof is tari is her word accepted unconditionally.
The text explicitly states 'because the qati' is tari'.
3
Apply the principle to the case
Since the slave girl states she was freed by her master, the proof of her slavery is tari (intervening) – she is free at the time of the claim. Therefore, her word is accepted without further evidence, and any marriage contracted on that basis is valid.
This contrasts with muqarin, where her claim would not be accepted without witnesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باندی نے کہا کہ میرے مولیٰ نے مجھے آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا قول معتبر ہے
وكذا لو قالت جارية كنت امة لفلان فاعتقنى لان القاطع طار ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر باندی نے کہا کہ میں فلاں کی باندی تھی اس نے مجھے کو آزاد کر دیا اس لئے کہ قاطع رقیت طاری ہے۔ تشریح باندی کہتی ہے کہ میں فلاں کی باندی تھی لیکن اس نے مجھے آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اس سے نکاح کر لینا جائز ہوگا اس لئے کہ یہاں قاطع رقیت طاری ہے نہ کہ مقارن ، اور اس کا حکم ماقبل میں معلوم ہو چکا ہے۔
A slave girl said, 'My master freed me,' so her word is accepted. And similarly, if a slave girl says, 'I was a slave of so-and-so, and he freed me,' because the definitive proof of slavery is intervening. Translation: And similarly, if a slave girl said, 'I was a slave of so-and-so, he freed me,' because the qati' of slavery is tari. Explanation: The slave girl says, 'I was a slave of so-and-so but he freed me,' so her word is accepted and it is permissible to marry her because here the qati' of slavery is tari, not muqarin, and its ruling has been previously established.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
The same principle applies to a slave girl claiming freedom. If she says she was enslaved but later freed, her statement is accepted because the qati' of her slavery is tari (intervening) — she was free at the time of contract. Therefore, a man can marry her based on her word. This is in contrast to a concurrent qati' where such a claim would not be accepted without full evidence.

Key Terms
قاطع رقیت (qati' al-riqqiyyah): Definitive proof of slavery status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because she is trustworthy.
C) Because her master is absent.
D) Because she has a document.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave girl's claim of emancipation is accepted without witnesses if the proof of her slavery is intervening (tari), making marriage to her permissible.

News of Marriage's Invalidity Not Accepted
Ruling on accepting news of marriage invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a single informant's claim of a concurrent (muqarin) marriage defect is not accepted, and distinguish between tari and muqarin defects.

Core Idea
A claim that a marriage was invalid from its origin requires full testimony; a single informant's word is insufficient.

In previous lessons, we saw that news of a subsequent defect (tari) like later apostasy may be accepted from a single informant. But when the defect is alleged to have existed at the time of the contract (muqarin), such as the husband being an apostate or a foster brother at the time of marriage, the Hanafi school holds that the informant's statement is not reliable. This lesson explains why and when a woman should not act on such news.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a woman being informed by a single person that her marriage was invalid from the start. The ruling is that the informant's statement is not accepted; the marriage remains valid.
2
Understand the type of defect
The defects listed — invalid marriage (nikah fasid), husband apostate at time of marriage, foster brother — all relate to the origin of the marriage. Such defects are called muqarin (concurrent) because they existed at the time of the contract.
Contrast with tari defects that arise after the contract.
3
Contrast with tari defects
In earlier rulings, news of a subsequent defect (e.g., husband apostatizing after marriage) was accepted from a single informant. Here, because the defect is concurrent, the evidentiary standard is higher.
Muqarin defects require the same evidence as the contract itself — two male witnesses or one male and two females.
4
Apply the principle
If a woman is told 'your husband was an apostate at the time of marriage' or 'he is your foster brother,' she should not immediately separate or act. She must seek full testimony from two just witnesses.
This protects the stability of marriage from unsubstantiated claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے عورت کو خبر دی کہ تمہارا نکاح فاسد تھا یا بوقت نکاح شوہر مرتد تھا یا اس کا رضاعی بھائی ہے تو مخبر کا قول معتبر نہیں سمجھا جائے گا
ولو اخبرها مخبر ان اصل النكاح كان فاسدا او كان الزوج حين تزوجها مرتداً اواخاها من الرضاعة
And if an informant informs a woman that the original marriage was invalid, or that the husband at the time of marriage was an apostate, or that he is her foster brother, then the informant's statement is not considered reliable.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This ruling states the opposite of the previous ones. If someone informs a woman that her existing marriage is fundamentally invalid (due to the husband being an apostate or being a foster relative), that informant's word is not accepted. Why? Because here the qati' that invalidates the marriage would be muqarin (concurrent) — it relates to the very foundation of the contract. Such a claim requires the full testimony of two just witnesses, not just a single informant.

Key Terms
نکاح فاسد (nikah fasid): An invalid marriage due to a defect in its foundation.
مرتد (murtadd): Apostate; a Muslim who renounces Islam. Marriage with an apostate is invalid.
رضاعی بھائی (rada'i bhai): Foster brother; a man breastfed by the same woman, making him a mahram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the news is important.
B) No, because the defect is concurrent (muqarin) requiring full evidence.
C) Yes, if he is trustworthy.
D) No, because women's marriages are always valid.

Key Takeaway
A single informant's claim that a marriage was originally invalid is not accepted; the marriage remains valid pending full evidence.

The Principle of Qati' Tari: Accepting a Single Report for Supervening Impediments
Defines qati' tari and muqarin and applies to three cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qati' tari and qati' muqarin and apply the Hanafi rule that a single trustworthy report suffices for supervening impediments.

Core Idea
A supervening impediment is proven by one trustworthy person; a concurrent impediment needs full testimony.

In Hanafi fiqh, the nature of an impediment (qati') determines the evidence required. This passage explains that if the impediment arises after the contract (tari), a single reliable report is accepted, especially if supported by predominant conjecture. In contrast, if it existed from the start (muqarin), formal testimony is needed. We will explore the principle and its application in three key marriage scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of impediments
Distinguish between supervening (tari) and concurrent (muqarin) based on their timing relative to the contract.
Tari occurs after the contract; muqarin was present from the start.
2
State the evidence rule for each category
For tari: one trustworthy person, or an untrustworthy person if supported by predominant conjecture. For muqarin: full shahadat (two males or one male and two females).
The rule is based on the principle that supervening matters are more easily proven.
3
Apply the rule to the woman's report of divorce
If a woman says her husband divorced her and her iddah is over, and there is no opposing evidence, her report is accepted and the man may marry her.
The condition is that the hearer has predominant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann) of her truthfulness.
4
Apply to the thrice-divorced woman's remarriage scenario
A thrice-divorced woman reports that she married another man, consummated, was divorced, and her iddah passed; the first husband may remarry her based on her report.
Again, no opposing factor (mu'arid) is present.
5
Apply to the slave-girl's report of manumission
A slave-girl says her master freed her; her statement is accepted because the impediment of slavery is supervening (tari) – it is removed after the fact.
This allows her to marry based on her own report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طاری کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ یہ قاطع پہلے سے نہیں بعد میں عارض ہوا ہے اور مقارن کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ قاطع عقد پہلے ہی سے موجود ہے گویا عقد سرے سے فاسد ہے اور اصول یہ مقرر ہے کہ قاطع طاری میں ایک ثقہ معتبر ہوتا ہے یا غیر ثقہ کا جب کہ ظن غالب سے اس کی تائید ہو جائے ، اور قاطع مقارن کے اندر نصاب شہادت مع عدالت کے ضروری ہے ورنہ ایک آدمی کا قول اس میں معتبر نہ ہوگا۔ عورت نے ایک شخص کو کہا کہ میرے شوہر نے مجھے طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گذر گئی تو شخص نکاح کرسکتا ہے

وكذا لو قالت لرجل طلقني زوجي وانقضت عدتى فلا بأس أن يتزوجها ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر عورت نے کہا کسی شخص سے کہ میرے شوہر نے مجھ کو طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گزرگئی تو کوئی حرج نہیں کہ وہ اس سے نکاح کرے۔ تشریح کیوں کہ کوئی معارض نہیں ہے لیکن اس کا یہ حکم جب ہے کہ اس کا ظن غالب یہ ہو کہ یہ بچی ہے، محمد یوسف عفرلہ ۔ مطلقہ ثلث نے کہا کہ میری عدت گزرگئی میں نے شوہر ثانی سے نکاح کیا اس نے دخول کیا

پھر طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گذر چکی ہے زوج اول نکاح کر سکتا ہے

و كذا اذا قالت المطلقة الثلث انقضت عدتى وتزوجت بزوج اخر ودخل بي ثم طلقني و انقضت عدتی فلا باس بان يتزوجها الزوج الأول

ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی جبکہ مطلقہ ثلثہ نے کہا کہ میری عدت گزر گئی اور میں نے دوسرے شوہر سے نکاح کر لیا اور اس نے میرے ساتھ دخول کیا پھر مجھ کو طلاق دیدی اور میری عدت گزرگئی تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے کہ اس سے زوج اول نکاح کرے۔

تشریح کیوں کہ یہاں بھی اس کی خبر کا کوئی معارض نہیں ہے اور منارع نہیں ہے۔

باندی نے کہا کہ میرے مولیٰ نے مجھے آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا قول معتبر ہے

وكذا لو قالت جارية كنت امة لفلان فاعتقنى لان القاطع طا ترجمہ اور ایسے ہی اگر باندی نے کہا کہ میں فلاں کی باندی تھی اس نے مجھے کو آزاد کر دیا اس لئے کہ قاطع رقیت طاری ہے۔ تشریح باندی کہتی ہے کہ میں فلاں کی باندی تھی لیکن اس نے مجھے آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اس سے نکاح کر لینا جائز ہوگا اس لئے کہ یہاں قاطع رقیت طاری ہے نہ کہ مقارن ، اور اس کا حکم ماقبل میں معلوم ہو چکا ہے۔
The meaning of 'tari' is that the interrupting factor was not present from the beginning but occurred later. The meaning of 'muqarin' is that the interrupting factor was present from the start, so the contract is essentially invalid. The established principle is that in a supervening impediment (tari), one trustworthy person is accepted, or an untrustworthy person if supported by predominant conjecture. In a concurrent impediment (muqarin), the full number of witnesses with integrity is required; otherwise, one person's statement is not accepted. A woman said to a man, 'My husband divorced me and my iddah has passed,' then the man may marry her. Similarly, if a woman tells a man, 'My husband divorced me and my iddah is over,' there is no harm in him marrying her. Explanation: Because there is no opposing factor. But this ruling applies when her predominant conjecture is that she is truthful. Muhammad Yusuf... A thrice-divorced woman said, 'My iddah passed, I married another husband, he consummated, then divorced me, and my iddah is now over.' The first husband may marry her. And similarly if the thrice-divorced woman says, 'My iddah passed, I married another husband, he consummated, then divorced me, and my iddah is over,' there is no harm in the first husband marrying her. Explanation: Because here also there is no opposing factor to her report. And if a slave-girl says, 'My master freed me,' her statement is accepted. And similarly if a slave-girl says, 'I was so-and-so's slave and he freed me,' because the impediment of slavery is supervening (tari). Explanation: The slave-girl says she was so-and-so's slave but he freed her; her statement is accepted and marrying her is permissible because here the impediment of slavery is supervening, not concurrent, and its ruling has been explained earlier.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage explains a key Hanafi legal principle regarding the acceptance of reports about impediments to marriage. The author distinguishes between two types of impediments (qati'): supervening (tari) and concurrent (muqarin). A supervening impediment arises after the contract, such as divorce or manumission, while a concurrent impediment exists from the beginning, such as the contract being invalid. Hanafi scholars rule that a supervening impediment can be established by a single trustworthy person, especially if supported by dominant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann). In contrast, a concurrent impediment requires full testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses. The passage provides three examples of supervening impediments: a woman claiming divorce and iddah completion, a thrice-divorced woman claiming iddah completion after a second marriage and divorce, and a slave-girl claiming manumission. In each case, there is no opposing evidence (mu'arid) to her report, so it is accepted. This allows the man to marry her (or remarry in the first husband's case) based on her word alone. The commentary adds that the ruling is conditional upon having a dominant conjecture that she is truthful.

Key Terms
قاطع طاری (qati' tari): An interrupting factor that arises after the contract, such as divorce or manumission, not present at the time of contracting.
قاطع مقارن (qati' muqarin): An interrupting factor that was present at the time of the contract, making the contract essentially invalid from the start.
ظن غالب (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant conjecture; a strong probability that the report is true, supporting the acceptance of a report from a non-trustworthy person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle explained in this passage, what is required to prove a supervening (tari) impediment to marriage?
A) Two male witnesses
B) One male and two female witnesses
C) One trustworthy person, or an untrustworthy person supported by predominant conjecture
D) The woman's statement alone, without any condition

Key Takeaway
A supervening impediment (tari) is established by a single trustworthy report or a non-trustworthy report backed by predominant conjecture; a concurrent impediment (muqarin) requires full testimony.

Nikah — Marriage

Concurrent Impediments in Marriage: Testimony Requirements
Explains testimony requirements for marriage impediments based on temporal sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for proving concurrent marriage impediments and distinguish it from supervening impediments.

Core Idea
Concurrent impediments require two witnesses; supervening impediments accept a single report.

The validity of a marriage is presumed from the act of contracting. When someone claims a pre-existing defect (muqarin), the presumption of validity opposes that claim, so full testimony is required. However, if the defect could have occurred after marriage (tari), the presumption does not negate it, so a single report suffices. This lesson walks through both scenarios and the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify concurrent vs supervening
Classify the claimed impediment: does it exist at the time of marriage (muqarin) or arise later (tari)? Examples: apostasy at marriage = muqarin; nursing after marriage = tari.
The distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the mu'ani' principle
The act of marrying creates a strong presumption (zahir) of validity. This presumption acts as a munazi' (opposing factor) against any claim of a concurrent defect.
If the defect were real, why would one have proceeded with marriage?
3
Apply testimony requirement for muqarin
For concurrent defects, full testimony is required: two just men or one man and two just women. A single report is not accepted.
This applies to claims of original invalidity, apostasy, or foster relationship existing at the time of contract.
4
Recognize the exception for tari
For supervening defects (e.g., child nursing after marriage), the initial marriage act does not oppose the claim, so a single report is accepted.
The exception is specific to scenarios like a small wife nursing from the husband's mother or sister.
5
Note the practical consequence
For muqarin, no separation or remarriage of relatives is allowed without testimony. For tari, the husband may act on a single report to separate or remarry within limits.
The text illustrates: for muqarin, cannot marry wife's sister; for tari, can.

بخلاف ما اذا كانت المنكوحة صغيرة فاخبر الزوج انها ارتضعت من امه او اخته حيث يقبل قول الواحد فيه لان القاطع طارو الأقدام الاول لا يدل على انعدامه فلم يثبت المنازع فافترقاو على هذا الحرف يدور الفرق ترجمه بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ منکوحہ صغیرہ ہو پس شو ہر کو خبر دی گئی کہ اس نے دودھ پیا ہے اس کی ماں کا یا بہن کا تو اس میں ایک کا قول قبول کر لیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ قاطع نکاح طاری ہے اور اقدام اول عدم ارتضاع پر دال نہیں ہے تو منازع ثابت نہ ہوا پس یہ دونوں الگ الگ ہو گئے اور اسی نکتہ پر فرق دائر ر ہے گا ۔

تشریح کسی شخص کی منکوحہ چھوٹی دودھ پیتی بچی ہے شوہر کو کسی نے خبر دی کہ تیری بیوی نے تیری ماں یا بہن کا دودھ پیا ہے تو اس میں ایک قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ یہاں قاطع نکاح طاری ہے اور پہلی صورت میں متقارن تھا ( کما ہوا لظاہر ) تو یہاں کوئی منازع اور معارض نہیں
If someone informs a woman that the original marriage was invalid, or that her husband at the time of marriage was an apostate, or that he is her foster-brother, his statement is not accepted until two men or one man and two women testify to that. Similarly, if someone informs a man, 'You married her while she was an apostate' or 'she is your foster-sister,' he may not marry her sister nor four others apart from her until two just witnesses testify. Because he has informed of a concurrent (muqarin) defect, and the act of proceeding with the contract indicates its validity and the denial of its defect; thus the opposing factor (munazi') is established by the apparent state. Explanation: In all these cases the interrupting factor is not supervening (tari) but concurrent (muqarin). The principle has passed: full testimony with integrity is required. So until two just men or one just man and two just women testify, their statement is not accepted nor given attention. Here there is also an opposing factor (munazi') because if such were the case, why would one have proceeded with the marriage? Rather, this action indicates the marriage is valid and the defect is apparent. The informer claims that there was a defect from the start, i.e., a concurrent defect, so full testimony with integrity is required. Note: 'He may not marry her sister,' etc., because if the informant's statement were accepted, the marriage would be void from the start, and then there would be no harm in marrying her sister. Similarly, if the report were accepted, he could marry four others besides her, but since it is not accepted, he cannot marry four others besides her; he can only marry three.

Contrary to the case where the wife is a small child and someone informs the husband that she nursed from his mother or sister, here the statement of a single person is accepted because the interrupting factor is supervening (tari). The initial action (consummation) does not indicate its non-existence, so no opposing factor is established. Thus they differ, and this point is the pivot of distinction. Explanation: If someone's wife is a small nursing child and someone informs the husband that his wife nursed from his mother or sister, here the statement of one person is accepted because the interrupting factor is supervening, whereas in the previous case it was concurrent (as appears). Here there is no opposing factor.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on impediments, now focusing on concurrent (muqarin) defects. When someone informs a woman that her marriage was originally invalid, that her husband was an apostate at the time of marriage, or that he is her foster-brother, that person's statement is not accepted without full testimony. The same applies if a man is told that his wife was an apostate or is his foster-sister. The reasoning is that the act of entering into the marriage presumes its validity and opposes the claim of defect. The informant is alleging a pre-existing defect (muqarin), which contradicts the apparent validity. Therefore, the normal rules of testimony apply. The note adds that if the informant's word were accepted, the marriage would be void ab initio, and the man could marry the wife's sister or four others, but since it is not accepted, he is restricted to the normal limits. However, there is an exception: if the wife is a small child who nursed from the husband's mother or sister, a single report is accepted because the impediment (foster relationship) is supervening (tari) - it could have occurred after marriage, and the initial consummation does not negate its possibility. Thus, no opposing factor exists. This distinction hinges on whether the impediment is concurrent or supervening.

Key Terms
قاطع مقارن (qati' muqarin): An interrupting factor present at the time of the marriage contract, making it void from the start.
منازع (munazi'): An opposing factor or evidence that contradicts the claim; here, the act of marrying indicates validity and opposes the claim of defect.
رضاع (rada'): Foster relationship through nursing, which creates a marriage prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a single person's report accepted when a small child is reported to have nursed from the husband's mother, but not when an adult wife is reported to be the husband's foster-sister?
A) Because the child cannot speak for herself
B) Because the nursing impediment is considered supervening (tari) in the child's case, while the foster-sister relationship is concurrent (muqarin)
C) Because the child's marriage is invalid anyway
D) Because two witnesses are required for all cases involving foster relationships

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires full testimony for concurrent marriage impediments but accepts a single report for supervening impediments, based on whether the marriage act opposes the claim.

Testimony for Concomitant Defect in Marriage
Ruling on accepting report of marriage invalidity with concomitant defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement for accepting a report of a concomitant defect in marriage and explain why full testimony is required.

Core Idea
A report of a defect that existed at the time of marriage (fasad muqarin) requires full testimony because the marriage contract itself creates an apparent claim of validity.

In Hanafi marriage law, not every claim of invalidity is treated equally. The key distinction is whether the defect existed at the time of the contract (concomitant) or arose later (supervening). This lesson explores the ruling for concomitant defects: why one person's word is not enough, and why the marriage contract itself becomes evidence that must be overcome by full testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its trigger
The passage states that when a reporter (e.g., a third party or the spouse) claims the marriage was invalid due to a defect present at the time of contract (apostasy, foster relationship, etc.), the reporter's statement is not accepted unless two just men or one man and two just women testify. Determine what triggers this requirement: it is a claim of fasad muqarin.
The defect must be 'concomitant' – existing at the moment of the contract.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
Why is full testimony needed? The author argues that the very act of proceeding with the marriage (al-iqdam 'ala al-'aqd) is evidence that the parties believed it to be valid. This creates a 'conflicting claim' (munaza') against the reporter's assertion. The marriage contract itself is an apparent (zahir) proof of validity, so stronger evidence is needed to overturn it.
This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) or talil based on the conflict between the act and the claim.
3
Distinguish from supervening defects
The passage contrasts fasad muqarin with qati' tari (supervening defect). For a supervening defect, where the marriage was valid at inception but later something invalidates it (e.g., a foster relationship discovered later), there is no conflicting claim from the contract itself. Therefore, a single trustworthy witness may suffice.
This distinction is crucial for applying the correct testimony standard.
4
Apply the ruling to the examples given
The text gives two scenarios: (1) a woman is told her husband was an apostate at the time of marriage; (2) a man is told his wife is his foster sister. In both cases, the reporter's word is rejected until full testimony is produced. Also, if a man is told that his wife is his foster sister, he cannot marry her sister or four other wives until the testimony is established; he is limited to three other wives because the original marriage is still considered valid.
This shows the practical effect: the marriage remains intact until proven otherwise.

His statement is not accepted until two men or one man and two women testify to that. Likewise, if a reporter informs him, 'You married her while she was an apostate or your foster sister from breastfeeding,' he shall not marry her sister nor four others besides her until two just witnesses testify to that, because he reported a concomitant defect, and the initial act of contracting indicates its validity and denial of its invalidity, so the opposing claim is established by the apparent. Translation: And if someone informs a woman that the original marriage was invalid, or that the husband was an apostate when he married her, or her foster brother, then the reporter's statement is not accepted unless two men or one man and two women testify. Similarly, if a man is informed that he married her while she was an apostate or his foster sister, then he shall not marry her sister nor four others besides her until two just witnesses testify, because he reported a concomitant defect, and the initial act of contracting indicates its validity and denial of its invalidity, so the opposing claim is established by the apparent. Explanation: In all these cases, the defect is not supervening but concomitant, whose principle has passed. Therefore, the required number of witnesses with justice is a condition. So until two just men or one just man and two just women testify, their statement is not accepted nor considered. And here there is a conflicting claim, because if it were so, why would one have proceeded with the marriage? Rather, this proceeding indicates that the marriage is valid and the invalidity is incidental. The reporter says that there is invalidity from the start, i.e., concomitant, so the full testimony with justice is required. Admonition: He shall not marry her sister etc., because if the reporter's word were reliable, the marriage would have been invalid from the start, then marrying her sister would be permissible; similarly, if the report were reliable, marrying four others would be permissible, but it is not reliable, so he cannot marry four others but only three.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Disliked Acts deals with the acceptance of reports regarding marriage invalidity. The ruling is based on the distinction between a defect that is concomitant (fasad muqarin) and one that is supervening (qati' tari). When a report claims that the marriage was invalid from its inception due to a defect present at the time of the contract, the reporter's statement is not accepted unless two just men or one man and two just women testify. The reason is that the very act of contracting the marriage (al-iqdam 'ala al-'aqd) is evidence that the marriage was considered valid at the time, which conflicts with the claim of invalidity. This creates a conflicting claim (munaza'), so the reporter's word alone is insufficient. The explanation clarifies that the full testimony requirement (nisab al-shahada) applies here because the defect is concomitant. The admonition (tanbih) explains the practical consequence: if the report had been reliable, marrying the wife's sister or up to four other women would have been permissible, but since it is not reliable, the husband is limited to three other wives. This illustrates the principle that a report of a concurrent defect requires stronger evidence to overcome the apparent validity of the marriage contract.

Key Terms
فساد مقارن (fasad muqarin): A defect that is present at the time of the marriage contract, i.e., the marriage was invalid from its inception.
منازع (munazi'): A conflicting claim or opposition; here, the act of marrying creates an apparent claim of validity that opposes the reporter's claim of invalidity.
نصاب شہادت (nisab al-shahada): The required number of witnesses (two men or one man and two women) for establishing a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require two just witnesses to accept a report that a marriage was invalid due to a concomitant defect?
A) Because the reporter is unreliable by default.
B) Because the initial act of marrying creates an apparent claim of validity, which conflicts with the reporter's claim.
C) Because the Quran requires four witnesses for any marriage dispute.
D) Because the Prophet (PBUH) cursed those who report defects without evidence.

Key Takeaway
For a concomitant defect in marriage, full testimony (two men or one man and two women) is required because the marriage contract itself creates an apparent claim of validity.

Acceptance of One Witness for Supervening Fosterage Defect
Ruling on accepting report of fosterage when the wife is a small infant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between concomitant and supervening defects in marriage and state the required number of witnesses for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A supervening defect requires one witness; a concomitant defect requires two.

In Hanafi fiqh, the number of witnesses required to prove a defect that invalidates a marriage depends on when the defect occurred. If the defect was present at the time of marriage (muqarin), two witnesses are needed because the marriage contract itself contradicts the claim. But if the defect arises after marriage (tari), only one trustworthy witness suffices, as there is no conflicting evidence from the time of contract. This lesson explores this distinction through the specific case of post-marriage fosterage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize that the passage contrasts two cases: one where the defect is concomitant (present at marriage) and one where it is supervening (occurs after marriage). Focus on the supervening case: a small infant wife who suckles from the husband's mother or sister after the wedding.
The previous case (not fully quoted) likely involved a defect existing before or at the time of marriage.
2
Determine the timing of the defect
In the supervening case, the fosterage occurs after the marriage contract. This timing is crucial because the contract itself does not address future events.
The Arabic term used is 'qati' tari' (supervening defect).
3
Assess the presence of a conflicting claim
When the defect is supervening, there is no claim or evidence from the time of marriage that contradicts the report of fosterage. Hence, no conflict exists.
In contrast, a concomitant defect would conflict with the initial validity of the marriage.
4
Derive the witness requirement
Since there is no conflicting claim, the report of one just person is accepted to annul the marriage. Full testimony (two witnesses) is not required.
This is the Hanafi position, based on the logical distinction (farq) between the two scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If someone's wife is a small nursing baby, someone informs the husband that your wife has drunk the milk of your mother or sister, that person's statement will be reliable. Contrary to the case when the wife is small and the husband is informed that she suckled from his mother or sister, then the statement of one is accepted because the defect is supervening, and the initial act does not indicate its non-existence, so the opposing claim is not established. Thus they are distinguished, and this point is the basis of the difference. Translation: Contrary to the case when the wife is small and the husband is informed that she drank the milk of his mother or sister, then in that case the statement of one is accepted because the defect is supervening, and the initial act does not indicate the absence of suckling, so the opposing claim is not established. Thus they become separate, and the difference revolves around this point. Explanation: If someone's wife is a small nursing baby, someone informs the husband that your wife drank the milk of your mother or sister, then in that case the statement of one is reliable because here the defect is supervening, while in the first case it was concomitant. So here there is no conflicting claim, unlike the first case where a conflicting claim existed. And here, even though the husband entered the marriage, it does not indicate the absence of suckling because the suckling appeared later; at the time of marriage, there was no suckling. So where the defect is supervening, the statement of one is reliable; where it is concomitant, full testimony is required. The author expressed this with 'upon this point revolves the difference.'
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents a second ruling that contrasts with the first. When the wife is a small infant and she suckles from the husband's mother or sister after the marriage, this creates a fosterage relationship that invalidates the marriage. However, this defect is supervening (qati' tari)—it occurs after the marriage contract. Therefore, there is no conflicting claim from the initial marriage: the contract did not indicate the absence of future suckling. Hence, the report of a single just person is accepted, and the marriage is annulled. The distinction (farq) between this case and the previous one is that in the previous case, the defect was concomitant (present at the time of marriage), so the contract itself indicated its validity, creating a conflicting claim requiring full testimony. In this case, no such conflict exists. The commentator emphasizes that the key to the difference is whether the defect was present at the time of contracting (muqarin) or occurred later (tari). This distinction has practical implications for the number of witnesses needed.

Key Terms
قاطع طارئ (qati' tari): A supervening defect that occurs after the marriage contract, such as post-marriage fosterage.
ارضاع (irda'): Suckling or breastfeeding, which creates fosterage relationships that can invalidate marriage if certain conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two men or one man and two women.
B) Four men.
C) One just person.
D) No witnesses are needed; the husband's knowledge is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a supervening defect (occurring after marriage) requires only one trustworthy witness, whereas a concomitant defect requires two.

Slave Girl's Claim of Freedom vs Possessor's Ownership
Ruling on conflict of claim and possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave girl's claim of freedom when a possessor claims ownership, and explain the reasoning based on the principle of yad (possession) and conflicting claims.

Core Idea
Possession creates a presumption of ownership; a bare claim cannot overturn it.

This lesson examines a scenario where a slave girl, previously in the possession of a man who claims her as his property, later in another town claims to be free from birth. The Hanafi school rules that her claim is not accepted because the possessor's conflicting claim and physical control (yad) outweigh her mere statement. Understanding this ruling clarifies the evidentiary weight of possession in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their claims
There is a slave girl who was in the possession of a man (dhu al-yad) who claims she is his slave. Later, she tells another man that she is free from birth (hurrat al-asl). The second man wants to marry her.
The girl's claim of freedom conflicts with the possessor's claim of ownership.
2
Recognize the legal effect of possession (yad)
In Hanafi fiqh, physical possession (yad) is strong evidence of ownership unless proven otherwise. The possessor's claim is not contradicted by strong evidence here.
The girl's mere statement is insufficient to overcome the presumption created by yad.
3
Apply the principle of conflicting claims (munaza')
When there is a conflicting claim (munaza'), the statement of the person against the possessor is not accepted. The possessor's claim prevails because yad supports it.
The principle is: al-yad ammarah 'ala al-milk (possession indicates ownership).
4
Contrast with the previous case (no possessor)
In a prior scenario, the slave girl said she was freed by her former master, and her claim was accepted because there was no conflicting claimant. The absence of a mu'arid (contradictor) made her statement reliable.
The key difference: presence or absence of a conflicting claim from a possessor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If there is a small slave girl who cannot express herself, in the hand of a man who claims she is his, then when she grows up, a man meets her in another town and she says 'I am free from the beginning,' it is not permissible for him to marry her due to the existence of a conflicting claim, and he is the possessor, contrary to what preceded. Translation: If there is a small slave girl who cannot express herself, in the hand of a man who claims she is his, then when she grows up, a man meets her in another town and she says 'I am free from the beginning,' then that man is not permitted to marry her due to the existence of a conflicting claim, and the possessor is the conflicting party, contrary to the earlier case. Explanation: If someone's statement has a conflicting claim, then the statement is not accepted. Therefore, a small slave girl is in someone's possession, such that she cannot clarify herself, and that person says 'she is my slave girl.' Then when she grows up, someone meets her in another town and the slave girl says to him 'I am free from the beginning,' then the slave girl's statement is not reliable because the possession and his statement are conflicting with her. Contrary to what preceded: when the slave girl said 'I was so-and-so's slave and he freed me,' then her statement was accepted because there was no conflicting claim. Here there is a conflicting claim, so the difference between these two issues becomes clear.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario involving a claim of freedom. A small slave girl is in the possession of a man who claims ownership. When she grows up, she tells another man in a different city that she is free from birth (hurrat al-asl). The ruling is that her statement is not accepted, so the man cannot marry her. The reason is that there is a conflicting claim (munaza'): the possessor's claim of ownership contradicts her claim of freedom. Since the possessor has actual physical control (yad), his claim takes precedence over her mere statement. This contrasts with a previous case where a slave girl stated that she was freed by her master, which was accepted because there was no conflicting claim. The distinguishing factor is the presence of a conflicting claim (mu'arid). When no one else claims her, her statement of freedom is accepted; but when there is a possessor who claims ownership, her statement is rejected. This illustrates the principle that a claim against an established apparent fact (the possession) requires stronger evidence.

Key Terms
حرة الاصل (hurrat al-asl): Free from birth, i.e., never having been a slave.
ذو اليد (dhu al-yad): The possessor (one who has actual physical control over the slave girl).
منازع (munazi'): Conflicting claim; here the possessor's claim of ownership conflicts with the slave girl's claim of freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the slave girl's claim of being free from birth not accepted in the scenario described?
A) Because she is not trustworthy.
B) Because there is a conflicting claim from the possessor who has actual control over her.
C) Because she did not make the claim in front of a judge.
D) Because she was too young when she was in possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, possession (yad) is a strong evidentiary fact; a bare claim of freedom cannot override it when there is a conflicting claimant with possession.

Claim of Freedom by a Slave Girl: Contested Possession
Ruling on accepting a slave girl's claim when owner contests

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a slave girl's claim of freedom when the owner contests, and identify the effect of a contesting possessor on the admissibility of her claim.

Core Idea
A slave's claim of freedom is rejected when a possessor contests ownership; the presence of a munāzi‘ nullifies the claim.

In earlier lessons, we saw that a slave girl's claim of being freed by a specific person was accepted when no one contested. Here, the Hidayah presents a contrasting case: when the owner himself claims possession, the girl's claim fails. This distinction hinges on a key principle: the existence of a conflicting claim (munāzi‘). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of testimony and possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Describe the two cases: (1) a small slave girl in someone's possession claims freedom after growing up, and the possessor claims ownership; (2) a previous case where no such contest existed.
The passage contrasts 'bikhilāf mā taqaddam' (contrary to what has passed).
2
Extract the Ruling
For the contested case, the ruling is: it is not permissible to marry her. The claim of freedom is rejected.
The Arabic says 'lam yasa‘hu an yatazawwajahā'.
3
Identify the Effective Cause (Illa)
What makes the claim inadmissible? The presence of a contesting possessor (munāzi‘) who claims ownership. This creates uncertainty (shubha).
The text says 'li-taḥaqquq al-munāzi‘'.
4
Contrast with the Previous Case
In the earlier case (without a contest), the claim was accepted because there was no contradictory claimant.
Refer to the earlier part of Hidayah where the claim was accepted.
5
Summarize the Principle
A claim of freedom by a slave is accepted only if no one with possession contests it. If a possessor claims ownership, the claim is rejected.
This is a conditional rule: acceptance depends on absence of munāzi‘.

کسی کی چھوٹی باندی کسی کے قبضہ میں ہے اور اپنے نفس کا اظہار نہیں کر سکتی اور وہ شخص کہتا ہے کہ یہ میری باندی ہے پھر یہ باندی بڑی ہو گئی اس نے کہا کہ میں تو پہلے ہی سے آزاد ہوں تو باندی کا قول معتبر نہیں ہوگا
ولو كانت جارية صغيرة لا تعبر عن نفسها في يدرجل يدعى انها له فلما كبرت لقيها رجل في بلد اخر فقالت انا حرة الاصل لم يسعه ان يتزوجها لتحقق المنازع وهو ذو اليد بخلاف ما تقدم
ترجمہ اور اگر چھوٹی باندی ایسی ہو جو اپنے نفس سے تعبیر نہ کر سکے کسی ایسے شخص کے ہاتھ میں جو یہ دعوی کرتا ہے کہ یہ اس کی باندی ہے پس جب وہ باندی بڑی ہو گی تو اس کو کوئی آدمی مال دوسرے شہر میں پس اس نے کہا کہ میں حُرة الاصل ہوں تو اس شخص کو اس کی گنجائش نہیں کہ وہ اس سے نکاح کرے منازع کے متحقق ہونے کی وجہ سے اور وہ قبضہ والا ہے بخلافہ۔ اس صورت کے جو پہلے گذر چکی۔
تشریح اگر کسی کے قول کا معارض ہو گا تو پھر قول مقبول نہیں ہوتا لہذا ایک ایسی چھوٹی باندی کسی کے قبضہ میں ہے کہ وہ باندی اپنے نفس کی وضاحت نہیں کر سکتی اور وہ شخص کہتا ہے کہ یہ میری باندی ہے پھر جب یہ باندی بڑی ہوگی تو کوئی شخص ملا کسی دوسرے شہر میں اور اس باندی نے اس سے کہا کہ میں تو پہلے ہی سے آزاد ہوں تو اس باندی کا قول معتبر نہ ہوگا کیونکہ قبضہ اور اس کا قول اس کے معارض موجود ہے بخلاف ما تقدم “ اور جب باندی نے کہا تھا کہ میں فلاں کی باندی تھی اس نے مجھ کو آزاد کر دیا تو اس کا قول قبول کر لیا گیا تھا کیونکہ کوئی معارض موجود نہیں تھا اور یہاں معارض موجود ہے لہذا ان دونوں مسئلوں میں فرق واضح ہو گیا۔
And if there was a small slave girl who cannot express herself, in the hand of a man who claims that she is his, then when she grows up, a man meets her in another town and she says, 'I am free-born,' it is not permissible for him to marry her because of the confirmed conflict, and he is the possessor, contrary to what has passed.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a ruling distinguishing between two cases regarding a slave girl's claim of freedom. The author states that if a small slave girl (who cannot speak for herself) is in the possession of a man who claims ownership, and later she is found in another town and claims to be free-born, her claim is not accepted. This is because there is a conflicting claim from the possessor, who is the one currently holding her. The ruling contrasts with a previous case (discussed earlier) where the slave girl's claim that she was freed by a specific person was accepted because there was no conflicting claim. The key difference is the presence of a contesting party (munāzi‘) who has possession (dhū al-yad). When there is no contest, her claim is accepted; when there is a contest, her claim is rejected.

Key Terms
حرۃ الاصل (ḥurrat al-aṣl): A woman who is originally free-born, as opposed to being a manumitted slave.
منازع (munāzi‘): A contesting claimant who disputes ownership or status.
ذو الید (dhū al-yad): The person who has physical possession of an object or slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the slave girl's claim of freedom not accepted in this case?
A) Because she is too young to speak
B) Because there is a conflicting claim from the possessor
C) Because she was not freed by her owner
D) Because she is in a different town

Key Takeaway
A slave's claim of freedom is not accepted if there is a contesting possessor claiming ownership.
